FYI, the radium typically found in watch loom material has primarily alpha decay. Alpha particles (essentially helium nuclei) are stopped easily. You measured radioactivity through the crystal, which was mostly stopping the radiation from the loom (not sure about the bezel). 6 micro-sieverts per hour translates to about 52 milli-sieverts per year, which is slightly above the maximum yearly dose permitted for US radiation workers. It's probably not something you want to wear 24/7, but it's not the end of the world. There's obviously less radiation coming through the metal in the mechanism and out the back of the watch.

Bakelite is one of the earliest synthetic plastics, and it consists primarily of the following components:  Phenol â€“ an organic compound derived from coal tar or petroleum.  Formaldehyde â€“ a reactive gas that acts as a hardening agent.  When these two substances undergo a condensation reaction (usually under heat and pressure, with a catalyst like an acid or base), they form phenol-formaldehyde resin, which is the base material of Bakelite.  Key Properties: Thermosetting: Once hardened, it cannot be remelted.  Heat-resistant and non-conductive: Ideal for electrical insulators, handles for cookware, and early radio and phone casings.  Brittle but durable: Itâ€™s hard, dense, and chemically resistant.  So, in essence, Bakelite = phenol + formaldehyde + heat + pressure, resulting in a rigid, moldable plastic that set the stage for the modern plastics industry.

The glass is not cracked from an impact. It is called crazing. It happens with a lot of plastics. It could have been in the presence of some sort of gas in storage or it could be from the radium off gassing radon. I have seen plastics when sprayed with certain cleaning solutions instantly craze and then crumble/disintegrate within seconds. This watch has had a lot more time around where it could have been exposed to all kinds of things.

I do think there is some collector value to damn near anything this well made, rare, and full of character, but I think it's a shame how the collector market has turned what were well made working people's watches into insanely expensive display pieces. An apt comparison would be vintage Gibsons and Fenders. While nowhere near the amount of precision and care was put into their production, they were by and large reasonably affordable working musician's tools in their day. Now even 70s through to early 80s Gibsons, widely regarded as the roughest era for build quality and physically apparent shortcuts in construction, have tripled in value or more over the last 10 years purely because collectors drive the market up. It keeps objects designed for a purpose out of the hands of people who would actually use them for that purpose, which to me is sad.

This is something, as an enthusiast whoâ€™s recently decided to undertake the taking apart and restoring of a movement from scratch, piece by piece, I can relate to the feeling when you drop that seconds hand in and see it ticking and hear the mainspring working. I used a broken nh34 seiko movement as my brain as it was a very readily available movement and if something broke, another part could be easily sourced. I built the watch in the style of a (funnily enough) 6542! The cosmetics are in the style of a heavily faded 6542 to almost a coke look with a pumpkin lume. Normally Iâ€™m not a fan of fauxtina, but the the reasons I picked the case and dial is as because while the other bezels were ceramic(which ruins the vintage vibe for me), this bezel was a plastic replacement for Bakelite and the look and even the feel in the flesh took me by surprise by how consistent it felt to a real Bakelite bezel! But itâ€™s protected from becoming brittle and cracking so itâ€™ll hold up better over the years, and the dial was period correct in the hour and minute markings but I kept the dial 80% sterile to keep it from perpetuating(no pun intended) the idea that I wanted people to think itâ€™s supposed to be a Rolex, so I made sure no branding, or â€œoyster perpetualâ€ or chronometer certified were anywhere on it. I built it as a companion piece to the 6538 homage I built, which was my very first watch build. I can only dream of being able to sit and work on a movement such as this! But with my grandads 5513, I couldnâ€™t ever take the risk of giving my hand a go at servicing the movement. August 1967 I arrived in Chu Lai, Vietnam with the Seabees.  Our first paycheck in country we ran to the PX to buy items that were sold cheaply. Nikon cameras, reel to reel tape recorders, etc, etc. In my unit were a few Philippinos who elected to buy watches. I heard a group of them talking excitedly about what a great buy they had made. They were very happy to have purchased such a luxury item for a great price. I examined one of the watches, asked how much he had paid. He said $400 and I almost fainted. I had never heard of paying over $100 for a watch. I asked what kind of watch it was and he said it's a 'Rolex'. And I said, 'what's a Rolex?.

Just to augment your talk: GMT stands for Greenwich Mean Time, the Prime Meridian that runs through Greenwich In the UK, and pilots & air controllers work on that time zone regardless of where they are in the world -keeps confusion to a minimum as aircraft crews cross time zones. The 24 hour hand keeps track of GMT on the adjusted bezel while the pilot keeps local time or, as you suggest, the time at home.

Patina will come off the dial and bezel using the proper technique. I have detailed many high end vintage cars, planes, boats, etc. Magic eraser with soapy water works very well or a microfiber towel and some windex and or soapy water. I am not suggesting scrubbing at all but rather gliding over gently. Practicing on a less valuable watch like an old Gruen would give you confidence with this technique. If you much more prefer the patina then disregard. I prefer a vintage item that looks as perfect as possible, but I am a detailer so what do you expect.

I donÂ´t want to tell you this to ruin your day, but I cried like a baby when I watched this video. I lost my dad, 20 months ago, at the beautiful age of 90 years old and, despite the fact that he has been too many things during his life, his first and most important qualification was that he was a watchmaker. He was born in northen Portugal, in 1932, and learnt this office at 11 and 12 years old, during the WW II,  by watching his uncle (also a watchmaker) working on watches during the long and cold winter nights in the small town of Chaves, where he held his little shop, in the main street of those days. My father later on immigrated to Brazil, where he worked as a watchmaker for Rolex, among other companies, in SÃ£o Paulo, during the 50Â´s and 60Â´s. When I was born, in 1967, he was already a successful owner of one of the main jewelry shops in SÃ£o Paulo. Even then, he would do some of the main works himself. And I look at your equipment and I see you working and I can recognize some of the equipment he had and he used. Things for which I never knew name or use. Some of them I knew. Like the washing machine that you use at minute 20 in your video. I saw my dad putting pieces in those baskets in putting the baskets in those cilinders, just like you did, and I couldnÂ´t hold it. So, IÂ´m telling you that, no matter how important is this watch for their owner, you just brought back to me memories that are worth a lot more than those $100.000 this Rolex could cost. Hello can I send you mail   I have old rolex and I want to know about my rolex that's 30 year old rolex my dad wore
17:05 that sentence about being made for Pan-Am immediately explains both the previous paragraph and the name of the watch. For a pilot the most important timezone _is_ GMT. Because it never has summer time (Daylight Savings time) most commercial flights are actually scheduled in GMT (well UTC now, but when this watch was made that wasn't a thing yet). So a pilot doesn't have to know exactly what timezone they're in, they just look at the dispatch board and see that they're on the flight from Seattle to Tokyo leaving at 19:30 UTC, then look down at their watch with one of it's settings set to UTC and know exactly when that will be.
After disassembling, I would have to leave the country in a hurry, stepping on tiny pieces all of the way out.

Interesting what you say about the greases used in these Rolexes. My daily wear for the last nearly 50 years is a Rolex Submariner.  Probably made about 1972, serial 2635xxx. Bought from a pawnshop in 1975 for $200 Cdn! I used it for, among other things, celestial navigation (yacht racing offshore). It had recently been overhauled when I bought it. You must know that Rolex recalled these watches because the nuclear  regulatory authorities found that they were far too radioactive to be worn every day. I think it's safe to say that prior to your restoration, this watch had NEVER been serviced. In 1959 Rolex issued a statement recalling their GMT Master watches because of concerns from the Atomic Energy Commission because of the radioactivity of the bezels. That means that any GMT Master sent in for servicing after 1959 had their bezel replaced. The fact that this watch still has its original bezel is fairly conclusive proof that it was never serviced, as that bezel would have been replaced, since it's highly unlikely that a watch that's just a few months old (remember this was made in November 1958, so probably sold in early 1959) would have gone in for servicing.

One big mistake the people make when they're putting The Crown back on to tighten it down on the case you never ever for any reason just start to. Wind that thing back on clock wise because. When you? Don't catch the thread right? You immediately start stripping. The thread? Out what you have to do. Is you push The Crown in against the housing you back it up counterclockwise? Very? Slowly and not real hard and then all of a sudden. You will feel The Crown pop in toward the case that means you've hit the thread exactly as it supposed to be now you turn it clockwise and the. Thread catches and you never ever ruin the 2 and Crown. Some people want to just push it in and start turning a clock. Wise right away and that's how you ruin that entire mechanism. He had a full set of tools for fixing wrist watches, and cigarette lighters; manly stuff of the 60s and 70s. During our time these were everyday things. Certainly nothing vintage about them.   In fact, as young as 6, I had the â€œprivilegeâ€ of screwing in (or out) some of these marvelous tiny screws under his supervision. I also definitely had the continued â€œhonorâ€ of â€œlooking under the furnitureâ€ for some spring-loaded parts or the actual springs that flew off every now and then as he worked on these watches. I was the little sized apprentice.   While I personally havenâ€™t seen him working on a Rolex, he told me he did a few, before I was born. I did look over his shoulder as he worked on Seiko (Japanese) and Citizen (Swiss) and other big brandname watches of the time. My momâ€™s wristwatch was actually a Citizen.   It was a time when watches were valued by the number of Jewels (real or synthetic red ruby stones) used in their timekeeping mechanism. I have a Rolex 5513, bought second hand from an authorised Rolex watch dealer in London for Â£860 in 1986. There were many other watches on display in the shop window, however, most of the second hand (or used, or worse, the snobbish 'pre-owned') watches had a cyclops 'bulge' and I didn't like that look at all. Mine was just your bulk standard Rolex 5513 stainless steel submariner, and as I was, at the time, a serving submariner in the Royal Navy all aspects of THAT watch said BUY ME, so I did. Over the years, and yeah, it's been ALL over the planet and in most places where sea water meets coastlines, the watch did get battered about from daily life underwater, and it badly needed servicing.Â  Instead of doing the necessary I put it away in a drawer in my house and forgot about it, that was in 1994. In 2023 I bit the bullet and send my watch off for servicing at an authorised Rolex servicing workshop. Off it went in September 2023 and December 2023 AND Â£1,540 later I get the watch back from a jewellers in Hereford. Several bits and bobs needed replacing, wheels, winding crown, new main spring, crystal and all the O seals etc etc. AND it's been on my wrist since. I have my old watch back, the weight is very welcome, I'd forgotten the sheer bulky mass of Rolex watches. On the internet the word is that an 'officially certified chronometer' is considered acceptable if it's plus or minus 4 seconds either side of zero in one day. My bulk standard 5513 'non chronometer' 1530 movement is accurate to within plus or minus .5  (half) of a second. The watch was never as accurate as this since the day I bought it. It was manufactured in 1976, according to the serial numbers on the case, and is now classed as vintage.Â  Bought for Â£860, serviced for Â£1,540 = Â£2,400 which can seem a bit eye-watering - however, the minimum price that these watches sell for is Â£4,000 and the price continues to go up. My watch will never be sold.     Watching this vid laid bare all the skill and care that goes into taking apart, and rebuilding these watches. A real artisan was seen at work. And the absolute genius that goes into the 'gubbins' (one of my dearly departed dad's expressions) is astounding. All that expertise is inside may watch. It was on my wrist when I played cricket at the North Pole, the Arctic Ice Pilot, an American, advised us not to wear bare metal against skin, pah, what do they know, a lot as it happens - and a flash burn for me, live and learn then.Â  Marshall, cheers for the vid guy, and watch out for those 'flying' bits and bobs, pinggggggggg - damn, where'd that go?

Anyone know why he doesn't time the hands? From what I know, when setting hands, you typically go in this order: 1. Insert crown and stem -> 2. Rotate until the date _just_ clicks over to the next day, so it is exactly midnight/12:00am -> 3. Set all of the hands pointing perfectly at the 12:00 position so that the time is guaranteed to be 12:00, and thus as the seconds and minutes progress, they do so in sync, and when the date clicks over, all hands will be again at exactly the 12:00 position. If not, the date will switch over seconds/minutes/hours ahead of or before the correct time (although it is typically just seconds).  He's obviously a master at what he does and knows more than I do, so if anyone could fill me in on why he didn't do it, please let me know. I would be greatly appreciative! *Edit:* If you pause it @51:45, set the video speed to 0.25x, and watch carefully or hit the spacebar a bunch to pause/unpause it quickly, you can see the hands line up directly at 12:00 as they should when the date switches over, but it's about 15 seconds fast. This means the minute hand will move one minute when the second hand is at the 3:00 position, not directly at 12:00.

Before the commercialization of the printed circuit board and the solid-state transistor, electronics were such an absolute mess of wires and components flapping in the breeze - in devices the size of toasters or fridges or entire rooms - that it's kind of crazy that they managed to function at all. And I say this as someone who works in electronics. Sure, some *_65 years_* after this Rolex was put together, we have CAD, microlithography, wave-soldering, and printed circuit boards with multiple layers, and machines built with these tools that are able to perform timekeeping and calculations on a nanosecond scale... but, it's still so awe-inspiring how people managed to build such precise mechanical timekeeping devices on the scale of a silver dollar so many decades ago. I think back to the late 1700s and 1800s when people were fooled by The Turk, a supposed clockwork-based chess-playing computer, and I can hardly blame them.

Well done, thanks for a beautiful video.
RE radiation: CPM (counts per minute) is a bit misleading where it comes to the danger level of the radioactive material (yes, I know its radium, and am aware of its history as well as the modern millennial scaremongering regarding radium dials, fiestaware, etc. Im speaking about general nuclear radioactivity knowledge, calm down sparky...). Its literally trigger instances of the detector. The static coming off your body could trigger a 'count'. Also, depending on what the sensor is made to detect, it could be effectively harmless alpha, it could be low energy beta, or high energy beta, or gamma.....it doesnt say. Its just a 'count'. And counts DO NOT IN ANY WAY directly relate to the important measurements of radiation, the important ones to pay attention to is RAD and the REM. Radiation Absorbed Dose, and Roentgen Equivalent Man. Those directly measure the damage done by being exposed to harmful radiation. Counts, Becquerels, Curies ONLY MEASURE ATOMIC EVENTS!!!! And are effectively useless for determining damage to your person without knowing anything more about the type of radiation, beyond displaying that there is an atom undergoing radioactive decay. And there are tons of those naturally around and in you right now, all the time. Beware news outlets that use Becquerels, because they're scaremongering.  WR, on the other hand was demonstrating that there was radioactive material on the watch, and thus is not a scaremonger. However, with the nuclear radiation misinformation out there, I had to infodump yall.  Oh, by the way, 6 uSv (microsieverts, or 0.006 millisieverts ) is the equivalent of 2 days in Denver, CO. Ergo, remember kiddies, its neat when it beeps, but making it go ticky ticky ticky doesnt inherently mean itll make you sicky sicky sicky. Just....ya know....if your meter makes noise pay attention to it because its trying to tell you something worth knowing.....

Thanks so much for the great videos! I love watching them! There are 2 things that you are not aware of, and I wouldnâ€™t expect you to. The first is that the case back is stamped with a Roman numeral 2, meaning that at least the caseback was (allegedly) produced during the 2nd quarter of 1958. The second thing is that you are missing a VERY important piece of this watch (part number 1030 7005-1 Intermediate sole plate for self winding mechanism). Here is the information copied directly from a Rolex service manual: â€œRotor Unit Repairs This calibre has not been made for several years and was replaced by calibre 1530-1575. There are however substan- tial numbers of movements of this type still used today and in some early production models a correction is advisable. The sole for self-winding mechanism, part #7005 with is made of brass, and the reversing wheels, part #7018 also brass, provide a bad friction surface resulting in signs of excessive wear. This problem was remedied in the course of production and the great majority of calibre #1030 movements have a steel sole spacer, part #7005-1 (not illustrated in material catalogue). This part which is available from the Rolex Material Division is inserted over the sole and provides a complete solution to the problem. After having added the sole spacer, it is necessary to readjust the endshake of the intermediate rotor wheel part #7021 since it will have been increased by an amount equal to the thickness of the spacer.â€ Much love!

Off subject, though considering bakelite becoming brittal with age. Your encounter with the stuff presents a second factor that I cannot relate to: the roadium paint. There could be undwanted reactions to a process I have learned from others that share a different hobby than time pieces, though both trades share tools, and one can tell other makers from each other by the quality of their workmanship that went into each piece they produced. I like the thrill of the hunt for firearm accesseies from a certian ventage that are no longer imported to the US. One can findÂ Kalashnikov patteran magazines at shows or even pawn shops; they are rareâ€”not as rare as that watchâ€”but being produced during the Cold War, the Soviets, to save weight and keep material costs down, began to produce them from bakelite instead of the original blued steel. Well, as it turns out, and as you know, bakelite magazines become brittle with age. This is not a complete reversal of the dammage but rather a treatment and a stoppage of the progression that ultimately causes failure of the part. First, clean any oil from handling, storage, and solvents in a light detergent like Dawn dish soap (I like the new blue Gain's sent over Dawn as I prefer G-96 to the common CLP, I digress) and hot water. This process is important because next we are going to "rehydrate" the bakealite by submerging the whole affected piece that has become brittal in mineral oil. Time in the oil varies from piece to piece due to service history, storage, and handling conditions, but once cleaned, bakealite is a lot like a dry piece of wood, nature, or a tool with a wood handle that needs a "drink of oil" like we use lotion to rehydrate our skin in the dry winter months. Maybe you can acquire a suitable, but invalued, sample of the correct treatment and near ventage to do your own testing on, and hopefully this sudgested method bears fruit and brings you value in your hobby as it has mine. Who knows, it could become a thing that you can share for others to learn and help preserve the aging Bakalite items they encounter in their hobbies too. Merry Christmas, as the clock has passed twelve here in the Ozarks, and have a happy new year.

I'd put the another one, and it was successful. Also I put the LED for lightning up the LCD. What was amazing me with this watch, comparing with other watches, was the fact that this is the only watch, where crystal glass is used to emit a sound, so it using it as piezo buzzer! This way, under the water while you diving, you hear the sound, 3 times better, then when the buzzer is placed under the back cover of the watch (anyway, the sound under the water is spreading 3 times further than on air), so this solution was truly wonderful. As the watch has countdown timer so you can set it to count how much oxigen you have in you bottle/s, it's crucial to rely on his buzzing noise, when the oxigen level remains low. What a beautiful idea and engineering behind this. With his glass used as piezo buzzer, you're not gonna miss this information.

Based on your Radiac .5 mrem/hr * 2000hrs = 1000 mrem= 1Rem.  The IS legal limit is 5Rem per year. Meaning you would have to have the watch on for 10,000 hours to receive the max legal dose.  However there are only 8760hr in a year so your good.

Great work with your videos.  I wanted to let you know that virtually every non-quick set calendar mechanism does NOT require you to move 24 hours to advance the date.  Usually, all that is needed is to move the hands past midnight and back to around 8:30-10:00 (this varies by mechanism), past midnight again, repeating this cycle until the desired date is reached.  You will see the date number jump slightly when the mechanism has reached that earlier time.  This saves some unnecessary wear and tear on the cannon pinion.
Very wise to by a radioactivity meter. If I'm not mistaken Radium mostly gives of Alpha radiation so your meter must be suitable for that.Ingesting Radium is dangerous and wil mostly displace Calcium in your  bones , but far mor dangerous is inhalation of dust as local dose rate wil increase dramatically near the particle. You might want to keep your detector in a zip bag to avoid contamination of the detector. I suppose you can wipe dust with damp cloths and dispose of it properly.

About the dial: I watched to the end where you discussed that it appears to be a refinished dial. That matches with the appearance, and also illuminates a huge caution with these watches. The original dials had radium-painted markers which would deteriorate into dust particles that would be very hazardous if you didn't take more precaution when removing the movement. They would have a chalky-appearance if original. This dial's numerals have a dull gloss, which indicates a refinish.

Small note about the rubies. Molecularly, synthetic rubies are the same as natural rubies. However, they are distinguishable under a microscope because synthetic gems have curved striations whereas natural gems' striations are perfectly straight. This is due to how the gems are "grown" vs formed in nature.

I can relate in a way. Working on priceless things or things that mean everything to the owner can be extremely intimidating. In the early 80's I worked as a car detailer and PDI technician at a new car dealership. The owner was a really tough customer. Not a very nice guy. His father owned an original, unrestored 1953 Corvette and brought it to the dealership to have it cleaned and detailed prior to a very prestigious car show he was taking it to the following week. I have no idea which show he was taking it to. They brought it out to me, told me not to screw anything up, turned and left. 10 hours of constant sweat and terror later I had the car done. They walked around it for at least 20 minutes before the father nodded and had some guys load it on a trailer. He then turned to me, smiled and handed me 3 crisp new one hundred dollar bills. I was a bit shocked but grateful none the less. The guy never said a word to me the whole time. I remember seeing a car like that sell on Barrett Jackson Auctions for over two million!

Dad had gone to a jeweler but was told that it was going to cost a few hundred dollars to fix.  Well in the 1970s that was a lot of money and he just put the watch in a drawer.   Four years ago when I was moving our parents closer to us I saw the watch and asked my mom if I could take it and surprise my dad by getting it fixed.   The bevel was scratched, the watch did not work and the back cover had the inscription JC 1952.   I took it to a recommended jeweler, he replaced the bevel, replaced the back and the watch came back working but I noticed it was always 5 minutes off per day. I gave it to my dad for Christmas three years ago when he was 89 and he cried with happiness.   After all those years and he could finally wear it.   Dad died the following year and I inherited the watch and just put it in my safe.   Since watching your videos I now notice that the watch does not work.   I wish I could get it fixed and would love a recommendation in my area.

It might have already been said but the cracking in the crystal is actually crazing from the radiation emissions of the dial and hands. In 1961 Rolex was sued for $5.6m by USN Lt Cmdr Willard M Mound for damages to his skin and eyes which he claimed was caused by the radiation from his Rolex 6542. In the early 60â€™s watch brands stopped using radium and replaced it with the far less radioactive Tritium based materials.

Bakelite is a thermoset plastic not a thermoplastic. They are both plastics. Thermosets like Bake-a-lite, epoxy and polyester resins cannot be remoulded. However, thermo plastics can be remoulded. Like ABS, PVC Polycarbonate (PC). Uses for thermosets, pool balls, pot handles, camp dishes and cups, old electrical outlets, fiberglass boats etc. I could go on and on. Don't want to bore anyone.

As a machinist, metal is metal, Acetone or Methyl Ethyl Ketone is good to soak metal parts that have dried up oils, varnishes, O-rings and rubber seals. Alcohol is almost a useless solvent, it barely removes dirt. Dawn dish-washing liquid is more hot than alcohol by a long shot.

Great video!  I bought a GMT Master II "Coke" in 1999.  I had just entered my career and I knew I wanted a Rolex Submariner.  I strolled into my closest Jeweler that carried Rolex and looked for that submariner with the "cyclops."  When I saw the GMT Master II with the oyster case and bracelet, but with the red black bezel I had to try it on.  It fit perfectly on my larger than average wrist.  It came home with me after a $3,320 charge to my credit card.  I wore that watch all the time!  It was my daily for more than 10 years.  However, I would not wear it to the beach, or in the ocean.  I worked as a lifeguard on the beach / ocean front for dozens of summers, but I could not subject my GMT to the rigors of moving and using equipment e.g. boats, jet skis, paddle boards, ect. that was all apart of the job when not sitting in the sun.  When the red on the bezel faded I did not like that.  I had it serviced by Rolex and it came back looking like new.  I loved that.  Fast forward to five years ago when I realized that I "ruined" the patina, and the bevel edges were touched up.  The bezel was replaced, but thankfully the "swiss only" dial remained and so did the hands.  And I am completely happy with it.  In my opinion I "ruined" nothing.  I stopped the daily wear for a long time because I thought for me, it was too valuable to wear.  But I have revised this thinking, and it is now more often on my wrist than not, but just not at the beach.  I love and cherish it as I did that first day I took it home.  Cheers!

Hey friend, great video, love the details. I am a CNC machinist, and for a time, I was working for a Medical Device manufacturer. Our largest parts were tiny, about the size of the smallest screws you handle here in this video-and were largely platinum. Anyway, cleaning the parts in the baskets with (I assume) an ultrasonic degreaser-be careful putting them together like that. I erroneously left 100 parts in a basket down in the cleaning zone for 60 seconds too long one time and the entire batch had to be scrapped due to scratches. Not sure that things are so critical in watch making (scratches were only viewable through a microscope) but figured I'd still throw that out there.

While it won't compare to watch repair, many years ago I did subcontract work to repair office equipment of various kinds, and I was called to a university to help fix a "flatbed scanner" that had stopped working. I get there, expecting the usual desktop scanner, but instead they show me a custom-built large format scanner used by the university's Cartography Department, where they scan very large paper maps for digital storage. The scanner was all aluminum and about 6 feet long by 3 feet wide, and it was on a wheeled frame so they could easily move it around. I'd never worked on anything like it, but I eventually diagnosed that one of the motors that moves the imaging array had burned out, but while I had the thing pulled apart to remove the motor, I asked them what the scanner cost, and they said "$95,000", which took me more than a little off guard. lol  We obtained a replacement motor and I had it all put back together and working a few days later, although I was sweating just a little bit through the whole process.

You're one of the people adding mythology to those watches. No, it's not the most beautiful. It's just a very worn-out, quite old watch. Piece of junk wrapped in 100k. It is expensive on the collectors market, and would be bought to sell at even higher price in a few years. It's just an investment. The price isn't because it's so awesome and pretty. It's because of the crazy bubble, which will probably eventually break, like all the other collectors bubbles. Read about tulip mania, and in a recent years, a vintage video game bubble, which is breaking on our eyes right now. This was built in 1958 and been bought by a random guy, worn for years and then dumped into a drawer. When it was bought, it costed nowhere near current new Rolex prices. Again, because of the bubble and luxury market created around the business. Why was it dumped and forgotten? Probably because the service was not worth the money, the owner bought some electronic, better watch, and that Rolex wasn't worth big money at the time, so even servicing it and selling wasn't worth the time.

Seems as though radiation causes crystal crazing.
Absolutely loved this video! BTW the crystal bezel is an amazing example of what happens to Sapphire crystal exposed to radiation from the Radon. the Radiation causes the crystal to concentrate heat on the side the crystal is most exposed to it. the radon gas and whatever sun it received caused the internal of the crystal to increase in heat and cause cracks thorough the structure from thermal expansion and contraction.
36:48 - isopropyl alcohol is pretty good solvent, but if you are looking for REALLY good solvent you may want to check out cyclohexanone. Boy, it really does miracles - it can even dissolve solidified oil paint (the one they used on old paintings) or hardened boiled linseed oil. Speaking of which, you mentioned earlier "dried (out) oils" (oils they used earlier to lubricate watches) - strictly speaking those oils (fats) don't "dry", they "set" or "harden" - just like gypsum or concrete doesn't just "dry" (although it becomes dry after pouring/ plastering onto a wall), they SET hard. For natural oils, or "fats", aka triglycerides (i..e esters of glycerine and higher fatty acids), the process involves oxygen from the air oxidising them and cross-linking of molecules, making them essentially insoluble in any "regular" organic solvent. However, cyclohexanone (an alicyclic ketone, a cousin of regular acetone, the simplest member of ketones family) can actually penetrate the structure of hardened oils/ fats and dissolve it.  In this case I'd bet if you just place that watch case in cyclohexanone overnight the entire seal would either be dissolved or turned into a soft jelly - so a simple swipe with a bamboo toothpick would remove it completely. But keep in mind that cyclohexanone has a very distinctive and VERY strong smell - not strictly unpleasant, but it tends to fill the air pretty quickly and stay around whenever it is used. Some people may actually like it, some find it annoying, but it's not toxic and not irritating. It's just very intense and persistent.  It also does work great for old, stone hard dried out old brushes too- wet such brush with cyclohexanone, wrap it tight in aluminium foil, leave it for couple of days or a week, and then just wash with your regular "universal thinner" (typically made of/ containing acetone and butyl acetate).  Also, silicone grease - it's called "grease" but those silicone based compounds (like lab grade grease for grounded conical glass joints or silicone sprays) have somewhat different properties than "normal" greases. They are first and foremost anti-stick agents (silicone spray is a great thing for your car doors "gaskets" - spray them a little with it at the autumn and you'll never have to worry about them getting freeze-stuck to the car body!), and they do a good job as lubricants for all things made of plastics and rubber. Also, they prevent  ageing of rubber. But "metal on metal" grease? Well, not really. Cleaning really delicate stuff (like that bezel ring) - try ammonium carbonate solution, (NH4)2CO3. Works better than plain water, leaves no residue, as any ammonium carbonate left on the watch parts will slowly decompose into ammonia gas, carbon dioxide (alo gas) and water.

Ouch. Chemist here. It REALLY gives me toothache when I hear phrases like "highly poisonous radon gas". People, were you sleeping on your high school chemistry classes, or what? Radon is a NOBLE gas - that means, it DOES NOT REACT (at lest not easily) with anything, so it just CAN'T be "poisonous". Plain and simple, eh?   The issue with radon is that it is a radioactive gas (hence the name) - but not POISONOUS. Phosgene is highly poisonous, and so is hydrogen cyanide. Radon IS NOT. BTW, same goes for "radium" - and both names are derived from Latin noun "radius", meaning "ray". And yes, radium does produce radon, but the amount is so miniscule (and stretched in time) that you'd have to spent Elon Musk's fortune on such watches and keep them next to your bed for next couple of years to actually get any results.  In fact, some people actually go to caves containing radon (which is a product of uranium decay - U â€“> Th â€“> Ra â€“> Rn; youtube.com/watch?v=zZkusjDFlS0) as in small quantities it is actually beneficial to one's health (or so is believed, see "radiation hormesis"). Anyway, the "radon issue" is actually about PROLONGED and CONSTANT EXPOSURE TO IT, as in some places is seeps from underground, through cracks in the bedrock, and being an extremely heavy gas (nearly 10 g/ dm3, or "litre", or approx. 7.5 times denser than air) it tends to accumulate in cellars and other underground cavities. If not breathed in, CONSTANTLY, it's pretty harmless, as alpha rays (emitted by both radon and radium, and while at it, polonium too) are stopped by few centimetres of air - or a sheet of paper.  When breathed in (or ingested, in case of radium or polonium) it does not "poison" you, it's just that alpha radiation, being a stream of relatively heavy and big particles (essentially, nuclei of helium gas) damages stuff in our body cells easily, so it causes this thing called radiation damage. NOT "poisoning". Or, to put it in more "American perspective and experience", it's like saying "he died of lead poisoning" when some unfortunate fellow accumulated few .44 magnum bullets in his body. Yes, lead IS in fact "poisonous", and yes, he's got more than a lethal dose of it, but "lead poisoning" wasn't the cause of his demise.  Speaking of radon, radium and watches - we all know the sad story of them Radium Girls, who would be perfectly safe and would live till their old age (bar car accidents or such) if SIMPLE safety precautions were observed back then - that is, "never touch this stuff with your hands, better yet use rubber gloves (disposable ones!) and do the actual dial painting in a fume hood like contraption - so any airborne particle of radium paint (highly unlikely occurence, still better safe than sorry) could not get on you". And then, just to be on a safe side, wash your hands after work, thoroughly. But (reportedly) they were told specifically to keep those brushes "sharp and pointy" (so the digits on dials will be nice and sharp) WITH THEIR MOUTHS. Now, it couldn't get any dumber than that, but hey, American education shining through. Or perhaps rather "lack of thereof", but who am I to decide...  Anyway, those stupid directives caused what they caused - still, the amount of radium/ alpha radiation those girls were exposed to would have blown this puny whatever meter of yours straight through the ceiling, so no need to worry about this watch. The fact that something is hundred times more radioactive than something basically non-radioactive (aka "at background radiation level") means NOTHING - it's like saying "a microsecond is thousand times longer than a nanosecond" - still, for us, humans, they both essentially means "very, very short period time". Did you know there's more attoseconds in one seconds that seconds since Big Bang? Or that bananas produce antimatter in your body? Or that the wooden chair you're sitting on may produce as much as fifty THOUSANDS of beta decays every second? No? So now you know... ;-)  Now, repeat after me, hundred times:  Radon IS NOT poisonous. Radon IS NOT poisonous. Radon IS NOT poisonous. Radon IS NOT poisonous. Radon IS NOT poisonous. ....... : )

Hate to be pedantic but....., radium decay is primarily alpha particles.  While the crystal on a watch cannot stop them, the human skin is very effective at blocking alpha particles.  The danger is primarily ingestion like the people who painted dials and hands with radium paint,  and licked the brushes to keep the bristles lined up.  Like anything, put a big hunk next to your skin, and you might get a radiation burn (Marie Curie).  Unless you swallow the dial or hands on that watch (or somehow inhale them), it is harmless. TheÂ Radium GirlsÂ were female factory workers who contractedÂ radiation poisoningÂ from paintingÂ radium dialsÂ â€“ watch dials and hands withÂ self-luminousÂ paint. The incidents occurred at three factories in the United States: one inÂ Orange, New Jersey, beginning around 1917; one inÂ Ottawa, Illinois, beginning in the early 1920s; and one inÂ Waterbury, Connecticut, also in the 1920s.  After being told that the paint was harmless, the women in each facility ingested deadly amounts ofÂ radiumÂ after being instructed to "point" their brushes on their lips in order to give them a fine tip;[1]Â some also painted their fingernails, faces, and teeth with the glowing substance. The women were instructed to point their brushes in this way because using rags or a water rinse caused them to use more time and material, as the paint was made from powdered radium, zinc sulfide (aÂ phosphor),Â gum arabic, and water. I'd suspect one of two things as the reason the crystal looks like that.  I've seen plastics deteriorate like that from exposure to strong solvents.  Acetone, MEK, things of that nature.    It could also be caused by 70 years of bombardment by radium radiation and exposure to radon gas.
That original crown was not stripped, that o-ring gasket did not belong inside the crown and was causing the crown to be unable to catch the case tube threads. I bet if you had removed that gasket and tried again it would have threaded perfectly. Rolex of that era only used two rubber o-rings and it was for  the inside of the crown tube and for the caseback.

Not just the radium is a problem with the bezel here. Bakelite contains Asbestos!
Rolex was not replacing the Bakelite bezels because of their fragility, it was due to the fact that the radiation the watches gave off with the luminous bezel inserts surpassed what was legally allowed by the US government, so Rolex could not legally send those watches back to customers with the original radium inserts.

Radium is a "bone seeker" that chemically can become part of the bone in your body and there is a small potential for bone cancer or other cancers in about 15 to 30 years after exposure. The Bakelite bezel creates a question. Marshal said the watch is from 1958. Rolex reference sites note the Bakelite bezel was dropped soon after the first year of production. A few reference sources say all Bakelite bezels were recalled due to radiation. (I wonder how effective a recall worked for customer watches out of Rolex control.) Hi Marshall, thanks for a fascinating video on a beautiful watch. I would agree with you that the dial is a replacement and not original -- note that there is no 'SWISS' at the bottom, no printed circle around the circumference, and the font is definitely dodgy. Just in case you didn't know, the 'II.1958' stamped inside the caseback doesn't refer to November of that year, but to the second quarter of the year (in Roman numerals). Thus you may see I, II, III or IV followed by the year. In later models the year is given in two figures only (e.g. II.68).

An explanation for the dial font variation might be down to the fact that Rolex used to change the dials as part of the watch servicing, and this could mean that a newer dial was fitted to the watch at the time of a service that had a different font to the original. I have heard this happen many times and the dial fitted would be the newest version at the time and not the original type. Delving into the service history of the watch with Rolex might reveal this, assuming it was done by an approved or appointed dealer. A truly incredible looking watch!!

You did not take proper precautions for working on a radium dial. Multiple times throughout the video you touched the radium surface allowing potential radium particulates into the air, with no apparent mask on, fume hood, or any other precaution. Especially when initially removing the crystal which had clear particulate matter on it, with a likelihood of containing radium. You let this simply drop from the bezel removal tool scattering it's harmful dust everywhere.  If you even so much as inhale a microscopic particle of radium, it binds to your calcium receptors, and remains in your body emitting radiation for the rest of your life. You should really take more precautions. This is why so many watchmakers do not work on these. Not to mention the illegal transport of this watch that most likely occured when it was shipped. The PPM being released exceeds every single commercial shipping company standard, these things have to be shipped professionally in radiation blocking containers, marked radioactive, and handled with care.  I love the restoration, but this video is reckless when working with radioactive material with a long half-life that also binds to your calcium receptors making it impossible to ever remove. The person that shipped this to you likely committed a felony (not that the feds ever care since radium dials are common on Ebay).  Also when removing the bezel and crystal, why not just put a pillow or something under your tool. You just let it plop onto the mat instead.

Marshall, this restoration was not a safe one for you. The radium paste/paint on the dial and hands can, after several decades, result in microscopic radioactive particles that once the watch is opened up, be inhaled. The strangely fractured plastic crystal might further be the result of 65 years of radioactive emissions causing degradation of the polymer. Hey Marshall!  Love your videos. With the crystal, it almost looks like chemical cracking to me. Certain solvents or acids can cause the material to develop cracking like this. I wonder if it took an accidental dip, or a cleaning was attempted using the wrong product, at sometime in its history.
Nice!
just food for thought. I have seen that kind of patterning on acrylic before. That was caused by solvents like alcohol, Lysol spray to be specific. Acrylic doesn't like those kinds of solvents, it tends to crack very nearly like that rolex did. It doesn't seem to be short contact, but longer term. The instance I'm speaking of, It was a small acrylic aquarium that stood off the surface it sat on with small bumps on the bottom. I sprayed lysol on the table it was sitting on to clean the table one day. I must've left a bit under the tank without realizing. The next morning, the whole front edge of the tank underneath was cracked almost exactly like that and leaking profusely. Obviously, I'm not saying that there's no way it cracked like that in any other way, but I wanted to throw it out there as food for thought as to possibilities how it COULD have cracked that way. Love your videos! Keep up the great work!
This hobby looks insanely interesting. If you want another desk hobby though that's a bit less finicky, more forgiving and cheaper then lock picking (and consequently, lock making) is also great. It was the gateway that got me to this channel.

Your comment about the condition of the crystal is spot on - unless it was due to a notable impact, it could also have been weakened by the extended exposure to the radium dial.
Rare as hen's teeth, and to find an original 6542 intact in the US, even more rare. There was a national recall of these in the 1960s because of a lawsuit by a gentleman claiming that the watch gave him and his family cancer, so Rolex went on to offer a free replacement of the bakelite bezel for one in metal. Taking into account how few 6542s were delivered to the US at the time and how few have survived over the years, this is TRULY an incredible find as this is also in an overall great condition. RIP to the original wearer and all the best to the future owner.

Funny, for over 25 years I never knew what those tools were called, a 'pen vise'. That was a standard tool we used at FDD-A(FL Detroit Diesel - Allison) since the late 70s till now even to removed terminals from older style Detroit connectors(and some really old Allison ones also), Now its almost obsolete, with everything being made by companies like Tyco(yes the toy company makes harnesses lol). I still have 2 or 3 in my toolbox, just collecting dust. They come in handy to hold needles if you need a really small pick for something.   Also, I didn't quite believe that watch was worth that much, so of course I had to look it up, online it is called the Pussy Galore watch. With all that Radium on the dial, I would not be surprised that the crystal was weakened by the acrylic cross-linking, the polymer creating longer chains by the ionization of some of the molecules bonding to other, likewise ionized molecules, over time. This creates a "harder" polymer, but with that increase in hardness, there is an increase of brittleness. I have seen examples of these effects on glass in a hazing of the crystal in a ring around the face of a pocket watch.

Marshall, I saw cracking like that on the crystal while in the Army.  We had shatterproof acrylic type windshields in our Humvees.  One night while deployed to Panama we were applying Army issue bug repellent on our arms, necks and faces.  My driver had some on his hand and smashed an insect on the windshield with his open hand.  We instantly heard a crackling sound and and saw a hand shaped pattern of cracks going through the entire inner layer of the windshield.  It looked identical to your crystal.  Just over a much larger area.  We did replace the windshield as soon as we could but the scary part was, if Army issue bug repellent could chemically shatter thich shatter resistant windows what was it doing to our skin?

A beautiful watch indeed, from the days when Rolex made really good watches without feeling the need to cover them with excessive decoration. Many years ago (in the late 1960s) when I still wore my Dad's 1946 Rolex, I was doing some volunteering in a hospital that was researching the radioactive implants that are now used to treat some cancers. Watches like this are soooo incredible.  I mean, you have to really appreciate even the smallest things.  Like how the crown wheel, instead of turning on a tiny narrow shaft, are made to slide around a (relatively) large diameter wheel, clamped by those two tiny plates.  This is probably because, next to the barrel itself, crown wheels have the most torque on them and thus the most side forces on their teeth.  Fun little thing about 'radium dial' watches.  When I was serving on nuclear submarine, they weren't allowed on board because of their radioactivity.  If a sailor's radium watch broke we would have to go through a whole 'radioactive contamination' clean up to deal with it so they were simply banned outright.  (well, the ship's divers had some for diving, but they were actually kept locked up except when a diver was actually going to go in the water).

Not to get too geeky, but I believe the properties that allows the cap jewel to stick to the setting are cohesion and adhesion.  The oil wants to stick to itself which is cohesion.  The oil also likes to stick to the cap jewel and setting which is adhesion.   Capillary action is a passing through of a substance through a medium.  Adhesion is the most accurate description. The cracking on the crystal like can be caused by exposure to a solvent such as acetone.  It partially softens an outer layer and the stresses within the plastic underneath this layer are given s layer that acts as a stress riser.  This results in the spider type cracking you see here.  It's a similar effect with tempered glass having a decent temperature gradient before spider cracking with no physical contact.

If worn 24/7, it would represent approx 56mSv for a year, which is basically 50x the max limit to absorb from an artificial source (because we also need to consider natural/medical exposure). For example, if an area is measured at 5,5ÂµSv/h (=50mSv/year), evacuation is strongly recommanded... In USA, exposure limit is 50mSv/year, all source included, (natural, medical, industrial, food, etc). Here, only by wearing this watch, you're ALREADY above the limit if worn 24/7...

If anyone is curious, the cracking on the glass is caused by a chemical reaction with a solvent that is not compatible, such as denatured alcohol. The solvent would dry out the top layer, causing it to shrink, then crank from the surface stresses.
